Source: 2026-05-31 Billion-scale vector search with Vespa — part two | by Jo Kristian Bergum | vespa | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Billion-scale vector search with Vespa — part two | by Jo Kristian Bergum | vespa | Medium url: https://medium.com/vespa/billion-scale-vector-search-with-vespa-part-two-bac817b30286 date: 2026-05-31T00:45:58.455Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap vespa On computing over big data in real time using vespa.ai In the first post in this series, we introduced compact binary-coded vector representations that can reduce the storage and computational complexity of both exact and approximate nearest neighbor search. This second post covers an experiment using a 1-billion binary-coded representation derived from a vector dataset used in the big-ann-benchmark challenge. Photo by Vincentiu Solomon on Unsplash The purpose of this post is to highlight some of the trade-offs related to approximate nearest neighbor search, and especially we focus on serving performance versus accuracy. Vespa implements a version of the HNSW (Hierarchical Navigable Small Word) algorithm for approximate vector search. Before diving into this post, we recommend reading the HNSW in Vespa blog post explaining why we chose the HNSW algorithm. Choosing a Vector Dataset When evaluating approximate nearest neighbor search algorithms it is important to use realistic vector data. If you don’t have the vectors for your problem at hand, use a publicly available dataset. For our experiments, we chose the Microsoft SPACEV-1B from Bing as our base vector dataset. This is a dataset released by Microsoft from SpaceV, Bing web vector search scenario, for large scale vector search-related research usage. It consists of more than one billion document vectors and 29K+ query vectors encoded by the Microsoft SpaceV Superior model. The vector dataset was published last year as part of the big-ann-benchmarks challenge. It consists of one billion 100-dimensional vectors using int8 precision. In other words, each of the hundred vector dimensions is a number in the [-128,127] range. The dataset has 29,3K queries with pre-computed ground truth nearest neighbors using the euclidean distance for each query vector. Vespa supports four different tensor vector precision types, in order of increasing precision: int8 (8 bits, 1 byte) per dimension bfloat16 (16 bits, 2 bytes) per dimension float (32 bits, 4 bytes) per dimension double (64 bits, 8 bytes) per dimension Quantization and dimension reduction as part of the representation learning could save both memory and CPU cycles in the serving phase, and Microsoft researchers have undoubtedly had this in mind when using 100 dimensions with int8 precision for the embedding. In the first post in this series, we discussed some of the benefits of working with binarized vector datasets where the original vector representation (e.g, int8) is transformed to binary. We don’t train a binary representation model but instead use the unsupervised sign threshold function. Using the threshold function, we convert the mentioned SPACEV-1B vector dataset to a new and binarized dataset. Both queries and document vectors are binarized, and we use the hamming distance metric for the nearest neighbor search (NNS) with our new dataset. Binarization using NumPy The binarization step packs the original vector into 104 bits, which is represented using a 13-dimensional dense single-order int8 tensor in Vespa. However, this transformation from euclidean to hamming does not preserve the original euclidean distance ordering, so we calculate a new set of ground truth nearest neighbors for the query dataset using hamming distance. Effectively, we create a new binarized vector dataset which we can use to experiment and demonstrate some trade-offs related to vector search: Brute force nearest neighbor search using multiple search threads to parallelize the search for exact nearest neighbors Approximate nearest neighbor search where we accept an accuracy loss compared to exact search. Indexing throughput with and without HNSW enabled Memory and resource utilization with and without HNSW enabled As described in the first post in this series, we can also use the hamming distance as the coarse level nearest neighbor search distance metric and re-rank close vectors in hamming space using the original representation’s euclidean distance. In our experiments, we focus on the new binarized dataset using hamming distance. Experimental setup We deploy the Vespa application to the Vespa cloud performance zone, and use the following node resources for the core Vespa service types, see services.xml reference guide for details: 2x Stateless container with search API (<search>) 2x Stateless container with feed API (<document-api>) 1x Stateful content cluster with one node for storing and indexing the vector dataset This deployment specification isolates resources used for feed and search, except for search and indexing related resource usage on the content node. Isolating feed and search allows for easier on-demand resource scaling as the stateless containers can be auto-scaled faster with read and write volume than stateful content resources. For self-hosted deployments of Vespa, you need to list the nodes you are using, and there is no auto-scaling. The following is the base Vespa document schema we use throughout our experiments: Vespa document schema without HNSW indexing enabled Evaluation Metrics To evaluate the accuracy degradation when using approximate nearest neighbor search (ANNS) versus the exact ground truth (NNS), we use the Recall@k metric, also called Overlap@k. Recall@k measures the overlap between the k ground truth nearest neighbors for a query with the k nearest returned by the approximate search. The evaluation routine handles distance ties; if a vector returned by the approximate search at position k has the same distance as the ground truth vector at position k, it is still considered a valid kth nearest neighbor. For our experiments, we use k equal to 10. The overall Recall@10 associated with a given parameter configuration is the mean recall@10 of all 29,3K queries in the dataset. Note that the vector overlap metric used does not necessarily directly correlate with application-specific recall metrics. For example, recall is also used in Information Retrieval (IR) relevancy evaluations to measure if the judged relevant document(s) are in the retrieved top k result. Generally, degrading vector search Recall@k impacts the application-specific recall, but much depends on the use case. For example, consider the Efficient Passage Retrieval with Hashing for Open-domain Question Answering paper discussed in the previous post in this series. The authors present a recall@k metric for k equal to 1, 20 and 100. This specific recall@k measures if the ground truth golden answer to the question is in any top k retrieved passage. In this cas,e, the error introduced by using approximate search might not impact the use case recall@k metric since the exact answer to the question might exist in several retrieved documents. In other words, not recalling a record with the ground truth answer due to inaccuracy introduced by using approximate vector search does not necessarily severely impact the end-to-end recall metric. Similarly, when using ANNS for image search at a web-scale, there might be many equally relevant images for almost any query. Therefore, losing relevant “redundant” pictures due to nearest neighbor search accuracy degradation might not impact end-to-end metrics like revenue and user satisfaction severely. However, reducing vector recall quality will impact other applications using nearest neighbor search algorithms. Consider, for example, a biometric fingerprint recognition application that uses nearest neighbor search to find the closest neighbor for a given query fingerprint in a database of many fingerprints. Accepting any accuracy loss as measured by Recall@1 (the true closest neighbor) will have severe consequences for the overall usefulness of the application. Vector Indexing performance We want to quantify the impact of adding data structures for faster and approximate vector search on vector indexing throughput. We use the Vespa HTTP feeding client to feed vector data to the Vespa instance. Writing becomes more expensive when enabling HNSW indexing for approximate vector search. This is because insertion into the HNSW graph requires distance calculations and graph modifications which reduces overall throughput. Vespa’s HNSW implementation uses multiple threads for distance calculations during indexing, but only a single writer thread can mutate the HNSW graph. The single writer thread limits concurrency and resource consumption. Generally, Vespa balances CPU resources used for indexing versus searching using the concurrency setting. Vespa exposes two core HNSW construction parameters that impacts feeding performance (and quality as we will see in subsequent sections): max-links-per-node Specifies how many links are created per vector inserted into the graph. The default value in Vespa is 16. The HNSW paper calls this parameter M. A higher value of max-links-per node increases memory usage and reduces indexing throughput, but also improves the quality of the graph. neighbors-to-explore-at-insert Specifies how many neighbors to explore when inserting a vector in the HNSW graph. The default value in Vespa is 200. This parameter is called efConstruction in the HNSW paper. A higher value generally improves the quality but lowers indexing throughput as each insertion requires more distance computations. This parameter does not impact memory footprint. We experiment with the following HNSW parameter combinations for evaluating feed indexing throughput impact. No HNSW indexing for exact search HNSW with max-links-per-node = 8, neighbors-to-explore-at-insert 48 HNSW with max-links-per-node = 16, neighbors-to-explore-at-insert 96 The document schema, with HNSW indexing enabled for the binary_code field looks like this for the last listed parameter combination: Vespa document schema with HNSW enabled The real-time indexing throughput results are summarized in the following chart: Real-time indexing performance without HNSW indexing and with two HNSW parameter combinations. Without HNSW enabled, Vespa is able to sustain 80 000 vector puts/s. By increasing the number of nodes in the Vespa content cluster using Vespa’s content distribution, it is possible to increase throughput horizontally. For example, using four nodes instead of one, would support 4x80 000 = 320 000 puts/. Sustained real-time indexing without HNSW, screenshot from Vespa Cloud Metrics When we introduce HNSW indexing, the write-throughput drops significantly as it involves mutations of the HNSW graph and distance calculations. In addition to indexing throughput, we also measure peak memory usage for the content node: Peak Memory Usage without HNSW indexing and with two HNSW parameter combinations. Now, you might ask, why is Vespa using 64G of memory for this dataset in the baseline case without HNSW? The reason is that Vespa stores the global document id (a 96-bit hash of the document id string) in memory, and the document id consumes more memory than the vector data alone. One billion document identifiers consume about 33GB of memory. Finally, there is also 4GB of data for the integer id attribute. This additional memory used for the in-memory gid, is used to support fast elastic content distribution, fast partial updates, and more. As we introduce HNSW indexing, the memory usage increases significantly due to the additional HNSW graph data structure which is also in memory for fast access during searches and insertions. Brute-force exact nearest neighbor search performance As we have seen in the indexing performance and memory utilization experiments, not using HNSW uses considerably less memory, and is the clear indexing throughput winner — but what about the search performance of brute force search? Without HNSW graph indexing, the complexity of the search for neighbors is linear with the total document volume, so that is surely slow for 1B documents? To overcome the latency issue, we can use one of the essential Vespa features: Executing a query using multiple search threads. By using more threads per query, Vespa can make better use of multi-CPU core architectures and reduce query latency at the cost of increased CPU usage per query. See more on scaling latency versus throughput using multi-threaded search in the Vespa sizing and performance guide. To easily test multiple threading configurations, we deploy multiple Vespa rank profiles. Choosing ranking profile is done in the query, so it’s easy to run experiments without having to re-deploy the application. Ranking profiles defined in the document schema. Exact nearest neighbor search performance versus threads used per query. As we can see from the figure above, one search thread uses on average 15 seconds to compute the exact nearest neighbors. By increasing the number of search threads per query to 32, we can reach sub-second search latency. The catch is that at as low as one query per second (QPS), the node would be running at close to 100% CPU utilization. Still, trading latency over throughput might be a good decision for some use cases that do not require high query throughput or where CPU utilization is low (over-provisioned resources). In our case, using all available CPU cores for our exact ground truth calculations reduced the overall time duration significantly. Approximate nearest neighbor search performance Moving from exact to approximate nearest neighbor search, we evaluate the search performance versus accuracy using the recall@10 metric for the same HNSW parameter combinations we used to evaluate indexing performance: max-links-per-node = 8, neighbors-to-explore-at-insert 48 max-links-per-node = 16, neighbors-to-explore-at-insert 96 We use the exact search to obtain the 100 ground truth exact nearest neighbors for all the queries in the dataset, and use those for the approximate nearest neighbor search recall@10 evaluation. We use 100 in the ground truth set to be able to take into account distance ties. With approximate nearest neighbor search in Vespa, the traversal of the HNSW graph uses one thread per search irrespective of the number of configured rank profile search threads; the threads are put to use if the ranking profile uses higher-level subsequent ranking phases. For use cases and ranking profiles without higher level ranking phases, we recommend explicitly configuring one thread to avoid idling searcher threads which are not used for the graph traversal. Recall@10 versus latency is provided in the figure below: Approximate nearest neighbor search performance versus recall@10. We produce the graph by running all 29,3K queries in the binarized dataset and computing the recall@10 and measure the latency for different run time values of hnsw.exploreAdditionalHits. The hnsw.exploreAdditionalHits run-time parameter allows us to tune quality versus cost without re-building the HNSW graph. As we can see from the above graph, using more indexing resources with more links and more distance calculations during graph insertion improves the search quality at the same cost/latency. As a result, we reach 90% recall@10 instead of 64% recall@10 at the exact cost of 4 ms search time. Of course, the level of acceptable recall@10 will be use case dependent, but the above figure illustrates the impact on search quality when using different construction HNSW parameters. Furthermore, comparing the 4ms at 90% recall@10 with the exact nearest neighbor search performance of 15000 ms, we see that we achieve a speedup of 3,750x. Note that these are latency numbers for single-threaded searches. For example, with 4 ms average latency per search using one thread, a node with one CPU core will be able to evaluate up to about 250 queries per second. 72 CPU cores would be 72x that, reaching 18,000 queries per second at 100% CPU utilization. Scaling further for increased query throughput is achieved using multiple replicas using grouped content distribution (or more CPU cores per node). See more on how to size Vespa search deployments in the Vespa sizing guide. Summary In this blog post, we explored several trade-offs related to vector search. We concluded that the quality, as measured by recall@k, must be weighed against the use case metrics and the deployment cost. Furthermore, we demonstrated how multi-threaded search could reduce the latency of the exact search, but scaling query throughput for exact search would be prohibitively expensive at this scale. However, using brute force search could be a valid and cost-effective alternative for smaller data volumes with low query throughput, especially since the memory usage is considerably less, and supported indexing throughput is higher. In the next blog post in this series, we will experiment with the original Microsoft SPACEV-1B vector dataset, using the original dimension with int8 precision with euclidean distance. In the upcoming blog post we will explore a hybrid approach for approximate nearest neighbor search. This approach uses a combination of inverted indexes and HNSW, which reduces memory usage. The method we will explore using Vespa is highly inspired by the SPANN: Highly-efficient Billion-scale Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search paper. Stay tuned! Last published Apr 22, 2024 On computing over big data in real time using vespa.ai

Source: 2026-05-31 Vector Databases Under the Hood — Part 2: How ANN Algorithms Make Similarity Search Scalable | by Aditya Ramaswamy | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: Vector Databases Under the Hood — Part 2: How ANN Algorithms Make Similarity Search Scalable | by Aditya Ramaswamy | Medium url: https://medium.com/@raditya.mit/vector-databases-under-the-hood-part-2-how-ann-algorithms-make-similarity-search-scalable-3be9346d4d41 date: 2026-05-31T00:45:41.945Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap
Introduction In Part 1, I stayed intentionally close to fundamentals, speaking about: vectors as representations dimensions as expressive capacity distance as the signal for similarity This worked beautifully. About this series This is part of my learning journey into vector databases — starting from the math we learned in college and working all the way up to production-grade systems. 👉 Part 0 — Why Vector Databases Exist (And Why AI Needs Them) | by Aditya Ramaswamy | Feb, 2026 | Medium 👉 Part 1 — Vectors, Distance, and Why Similarity Works | by Aditya Ramaswamy | Feb, 2026 | Medium Take a query vector. Compute distance with stored vectors. Sort. Return the closest ones. Simple. In fact, simple enough that most of us initially assume: “So vector search is just distance calculation at scale… right?” That assumption holds… for a while. It works when: you’re experimenting locally datasets are small latency doesn’t matter notebooks are your runtime But the moment you think like a systems engineer, new questions start emerging: What if I have 10 million vectors? What if each vector has 1,536 dimensions? What if search must complete in under 50 ms? What if queries are coming in thousands per second? At that point, the earlier mental model begins to crack. Because what we described in Part 1 is called brute-force similarity search — and brute-force does not scale gracefully. This part exists to explore that breaking point. Not from a purely academic angle, but from the lens of an engineer trying to move: from “it works” → to “it works in production.” We’ll examine: why exact similarity search becomes computationally expensive what “nearest neighbor search” really means in systems and why modern vector databases rely on approximation rather than perfection If Part 1 was about understanding similarity… Part 2 is about understanding how similarity survives scale.
The Limits of Brute-Force Search When I first understood similarity search, the implementation felt almost trivial. You take: A query vector A collection of stored vectors Compute distance with each Sort by similarity Return Top-K results That’s it. No fancy infrastructure. No indexing. Just math. And in small environments, this works perfectly. You can implement brute-force similarity search in a few lines of Python using NumPy — and it’ll feel fast enough to convince you that the problem is “solved.” But scale changes the equation dramatically. Let’s Put Numbers to It Assume: 10 million vectors 1,536 dimensions each (common in modern embeddings) Cosine similarity as the distance metric For a single query, brute-force search must: Compare against all 10 million vectors Perform a dot product for each comparison Normalize magnitudes Compute similarity score Rank results Even if one comparison is microseconds fast, multiplying it by millions turns latency into seconds — not milliseconds. And production systems rarely tolerate that. The Hidden Cost — Distance Computation Distance calculations aren’t free. Each comparison involves: Hundreds or thousands of multiplications Additions across dimensions Memory reads for each vector component In high-dimensional space, computation cost scales linearly with both: Number of vectors (N) Number of dimensions (D) So, complexity becomes: O(N × D) Which grows fast in embedding-heavy systems. Memory Access Becomes the Bottleneck At scale, the problem isn’t just math — it’s memory. Why? Because brute-force search requires scanning the entire dataset for every query. That means: Massive RAM reads Poor cache locality Constant memory bandwidth pressure Even if CPUs are fast, waiting on memory slows everything down. This is why similarity search is often described as: “Memory-bound rather than compute-bound.” Why GPUs Don’t Fully Solve It At first glance, GPUs seem like the obvious fix. They excel at parallel math — perfect for vector distance calculations. And yes,,GPUs help. But they don’t eliminate the core problem: Data still needs to be loaded into GPU memory VRAM is limited Full dataset scans still happen Latency SLAs still get tight at billion scale So GPUs improve brute-force search… …but they don’t make it infinitely scalable. The Production Reality Now layer in real-world requirements: Sub-100 ms latency Concurrent queries Streaming data ingestion Index updates Cost constraints At that point, brute-force search stops being viable except for: Small datasets Offline analytics Evaluation benchmarks This realization becomes the turning point. Because once scanning everything becomes impossible, the system must learn to: Search intelligently instead of exhaustively. And that shift leads us directly to the next concept: Nearest Neighbor Search. Not “compare with all”… …but “find the closest efficiently.”
Introducing Nearest Neighbor Search Once brute-force similarity search starts breaking at scale, the natural question becomes: “Do we really need to compare against every vector?” That question leads us to one of the most important concepts in vector retrieval systems: Nearest Neighbor Search (NN Search). At its core, the goal remains unchanged: You still have a query vector You still measure distance You still want the most similar results But instead of scanning the entire dataset, the problem is reframed as: “Find the closest vectors to this query as efficiently as possible.” What Does “Nearest” Mean? “Nearest” is defined entirely by the distance metric we explored in Part 1. Depending on the system, this could be: Cosine similarity → semantic alignment Euclidean distance → geometric closeness Dot product → magnitude-weighted similarity So nearest neighbor search is really just: Distance-based ranking in high-dimensional space. If you request Top-K results, the system must return the K vectors with the smallest distance (or highest similarity score). Exact Nearest Neighbor (ENN) The most straightforward implementation of NN search is: Exact Nearest Neighbor (ENN). This guarantees: 100% accuracy Deterministic ranking No missed neighbors And how is ENN achieved? By doing exactly what brute-force search does: Compare query against all vectors Compute distances Sort and retrieve Top-K So, ENN is not a new algorithm. It’s simply the formal name for exhaustive similarity search. Top-K Retrieval Changes System Design Another subtle shift happens here. In traditional databases, queries often look like: Equality matches Range filters Boolean conditions But NN search is inherently: Ranking-first, filtering-second. You’re not asking: “Does this record match?” You’re asking: “Which records are closest?” This ranking nature affects: Index design Query execution Storage layout Optimization strategies And that’s why vector databases are architecturally different from relational or document databases. The Tension Between Accuracy and Performance At this stage, engineers usually confront a tradeoff: ENN gives perfect results But latency becomes unacceptable at scale So the real systems question becomes: “How much accuracy are we willing to trade for speed?” Because if we can relax perfection slightly… …entirely new search strategies become possible.
Why Exact Search Doesn’t Scale By now, we’ve established two things: Brute-force similarity search is expensive Exact Nearest Neighbor (ENN) guarantees perfect accuracy So the obvious instinct is: “Let’s optimize ENN instead of replacing it.” Maybe better hardware helps. Maybe distributed compute helps. Maybe GPUs fix it. But as datasets grow, a deeper reality emerges: Exact search doesn’t degrade gracefully — it collapses. From Millions to Billions Let’s revisit scale — but stretch it further. Why does this happen? Because ENN requires scanning every vector — no shortcuts allowed. So latency grows linearly with dataset size. Double the data → double the compute. At billion scale, even milliseconds per million comparisons becomes seconds per query. And seconds are unacceptable in most AI-driven applications. High Dimensionality Makes It Worse Scale isn’t just about the number of vectors. It’s also about the number of dimensions. Modern embedding models commonly produce: 384 dims 768 dims 1,536 dims 3,072+ dims Each additional dimension increases: Computation per comparison Memory footprint Cache inefficiency This phenomenon is often tied to the curse of dimensionality: As dimensions grow: Distances become less distinguishable Data becomes sparse Indexing becomes harder Exhaustive comparison becomes more wasteful So you’re scaling in two directions: More vectors × More dimensions Latency SLAs Change the Game In experimentation environments, a 2-second query might be fine. In production, it’s catastrophic. Common latency expectations: Search autocomplete → < 50 ms Recommendation refresh → < 100 ms RAG retrieval → < 200 ms Now imagine serving thousands of concurrent queries with ENN. You’d need: Massive compute fleets High memory throughput Extreme operational cost Even then, tail latency would spike. So exactness becomes economically and operationally unsustainable. The Tradeoff Triangle At this point, system designers start thinking in tradeoffs. You’re balancing three forces: Exact search optimizes accuracy… …but sacrifices speed and cost. To make systems viable, something has to give. And in similarity search, that “something” is perfection. When Perfect Results Aren’t Necessary Here’s the key realization that unlocks modern vector search systems: Most applications don’t require mathematically perfect neighbors. They require: Relevant results Semantically close matches Good ranking quality If result #3 and #4 swap occasionally… …users rarely notice. But if latency jumps from 50 ms → 2 seconds… …everyone notices. So system design shifts toward: “Good enough results, delivered extremely fast.” And that philosophical shift sets the stage for the most important concept in vector retrieval at scale:
Approximate Nearest Neighbors (ANN) Modern vector databases don’t actually try to find the exact nearest neighbors. Instead, they try to find vectors that are… close enough. This is where Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search (ANN) enters the picture. What Is ANN? Approximate Nearest Neighbor search reframes the retrieval goal. Instead of asking: “What are the mathematically closest vectors?” It asks: “What are the vectors that are very likely among the closest — but can be found much faster?” So ANN introduces two deliberate compromises: It may not return the exact Top-K It may miss a few true neighbors But in exchange, it delivers: Massive speed improvements Sub-linear search time Production-grade latency Why Approximation Works in Practice At first, approximation sounds risky. If results aren’t exact, won’t quality suffer? In theory, yes. In practice, rarely. Because similarity search operates in fuzzy domains like: Language Images Recommendations Behavior patterns There’s no single “correct” neighbor — only degrees of relevance. So if ANN returns results that are 95–99% as accurate as exact search… …the user experience remains virtually unchanged. But the performance gains are enormous. Speed Gains Come From Avoiding Exhaustive Search The core optimization ANN introduces is simple but powerful: Don’t search everything. Instead, ANN algorithms: Partition the vector space Build navigable structures Group similar vectors Traverse likely regions first So rather than scanning millions or billions of vectors… …the system explores only a small, relevant subset. This is what allows ANN systems to achieve: Logarithmic or sub-linear search time Millisecond latency at billion scale Accuracy vs Performance — A Tunable Dial Another important shift ANN introduces: Accuracy is no longer binary — it’s configurable. Systems can tune: How deep the search goes How many candidate vectors are explored How aggressively pruning happens This creates a controllable tradeoff: So ANN isn’t just approximate — it’s adjustable. From Algorithm Choice to System Design Once approximation becomes acceptable, it unlocks an entire ecosystem of indexing strategies: Graph-based navigation Cluster partitioning Quantization techniques Hashing methods Each designed to answer the same quest,ion: “How do we avoid scanning the entire dataset?” And this is where vector databases start becoming deeply specialized systems — not just storage engines with distance functions. The Mental Model Shift If Part 1 taught us: Similarity = Distance And early Part 2 taught us: Distance at scale is expensive Then ANN teaches us: Efficient similarity requires intelligent shortcuts. Not random shortcuts but mathematically guided approximations that preserve relevance while slashing compute cost.
How Approximation Works (High Level) When I first heard “approximate nearest neighbor search,” my immediate reaction was: “So… the system just guesses?” Not quite. Approximation in vector search isn’t random. It’s guided. Structured. Engineered. The system doesn’t stop searching because it’s lazy — it stops because it has already searched the most promising regions of the space. To understand this, we need to rethink what searching a vector space actually means. Searching Everything vs Searching Smart Brute-force search behaves like this: Start at vector #1 Compute distance Move to vector #2 Repeat until the end It’s exhaustive. But also blind. It has no sense of where “similar vectors” are likely to be located. ANN introduces spatial awareness. Instead of asking: “How far is this vector from all others?” It asks: “Which region of the space is this query most likely to belong to?” Once that region is identified, search becomes localized instead of global. Partitioning the Vector Space One common approximation strategy is space partitioning. Think of it like dividing a city into neighborhoods. If someone asks: “Find the closest coffee shop to me.” You don’t search the entire city. You start with: Your neighborhood Nearby blocks Adjacent areas if needed Vector systems do something similar. They cluster vectors into partitions based on similarity. At query time: Identify the closest partitions Search only within them Skip unrelated regions entirely This reduces search scope dramatically. Navigating Through Neighborhoods Another strategy is neighborhood traversal. Instead of partitions, vectors are connected to other similar vectors — forming navigable paths. Search works like this: Start at an entry point Move to a closer neighbor Then to an even closer one Continue until no improvement is possible This is similar to navigating roads using landmarks rather than scanning every coordinate on a map. You’re always moving closer to the query, never randomly exploring distant areas. Probabilistic Exploration ANN systems also embrace probability. They accept that: The first path explored may not be perfect Multiple search paths may be needed Some neighbors may be missed So systems often: Explore multiple candidate regions Maintain priority queues of promising vectors Expand search breadth if recall needs improvement This balances speed and accuracy dynamically. Library Analogy — Exhaustive vs Approximate A helpful mental model: Brute-force search → Reading every book in a library to find relevant ones. ANN search → Going to the right section first, scanning shelves, then picking top matches. You may not inspect every book… …but you’ll still find the most relevant ones quickly. Why This Works So Well in High Dimensions Because similar vectors tend to form natural clusters. Embeddings trained on semantic data encode structure like: Similar meanings → spatial proximity Related concepts → shared neighborhoods Irrelevant items → distant regions So approximation leverages the geometry already present in the embedding space. It’s not imposing order… …it’s exploiting it. From Intuition to Algorithms All ANN algorithms — no matter how different — rely on some combination of: Partitioning space Building navigable structures Pruning irrelevant regions Prioritizing promising candidates The implementation details vary… …but the intuition remains the same: Search where similarity is likely — not everywhere blindly.
ANN Accuracy vs Speed Tradeoff Once I got comfortable with the idea that ANN systems don’t return perfectly exact neighbors, the next question naturally followed: “How approximate is approximate?” Is it 50% accurate? 80%? Do results degrade unpredictably? This is where ANN systems reveal one of their most powerful characteristics: Approximation is tunable. You’re not forced to accept poor accuracy for speed. Instead, you get a dial — one you can adjust based on system needs. Enter Recall — The Accuracy Metric That Matters In ANN systems, accuracy is typically measured using recall. Recall answers a simple question: “Out of the true Top-K nearest neighbors, how many did we successfully retrieve?” For example: Exact search Top-10 = {A, B, C, D, E, F, G, H, I, J} ANN search returns = {A, B, C, D, E, F, X, Y, Z, W} Here: 6 out of 10 are correct Recall = 60% In production systems, recall targets are much higher: 90% recall → good 95% recall → strong 99% recall → near-exact experience And often indistinguishable to end users. Speed Gains Are Non-Linear What makes ANN powerful is how speed improves relative to small recall tradeoffs. For example: So losing just 1–5% accuracy can produce orders of magnitude performance gains. This is why ANN is viable for real-time systems. The Tuning Knobs Behind ANN Different ANN algorithms expose different parameters, but the tuning philosophy is similar. Common knobs include: Search depth Candidate pool size Graph traversal breadth Cluster probes Exploration factor Increasing these typically: Improves recall Increases latency Consumes more compute Decreasing them: Speeds up queries Reduces recall Saves resources So system designers tune ANN based on application needs. Different Applications, Different Tradeoffs Not all systems need the same accuracy. So ANN configurations are rarely one-size-fits-all. They’re workload-aware. Index Build vs Query Tradeoff Another dimension emerges here. You can shift work between: Index construction time Query execution time For example: Heavier indexing → faster queries Lightweight indexing → slower queries So ANN tuning isn’t just about search parameters… …it’s also about how much preprocessing you’re willing to invest upfront. Visualizing the Tradeoff Curve If you plotted ANN performance, it often looks like this: X-axis → Latency Y-axis → Recall The curve is steep near 100% recall — meaning perfection is expensive. But slightly relaxing recall dramatically reduces latency. This curve is the economic justification for ANN systems. The Engineering Mindset Shift At this stage, similarity search design becomes less about math… …and more about SLO engineering. You’re balancing: Latency budgets Infrastructure cost User experience Retrieval quality ANN gives you the flexibility to operate within those constraints. Not by removing accuracy… …but by optimizing how much of it you actually need.
ANN in the Real World When learning about Approximate Nearest Neighbor search for the first time, it’s easy to think of it as infrastructure plumbing — something hidden inside databases and retrieval engines. But the more I explored modern AI systems, the more I realized: ANN isn’t an optimization layer. It’s the retrieval engine behind user-facing intelligence. Many experiences we interact with daily are powered — directly or indirectly — by ANN systems. Let’s walk through a few of them. A. Semantic Search Traditional search relies on: Keywords Exact matches Boolean filters But semantic search relies on meaning. For example: Query: “How to reduce cloud costs?” Relevant results might include: “Optimizing AWS spend” “Infrastructure cost governance” “FinOps best practices” These aren’t keyword matches — they’re semantic neighbors. Behind the scenes: Query → converted to embedding Documents → stored as embeddings ANN search → retrieves closest vectors Without ANN, searching millions of embeddings in real time wouldn’t be feasible. B. Recommendation Systems Think about: “Users also watched” “Products you may like” “Similar articles” These systems rely on vector similarity across: User behavior Content embeddings Interaction patterns ANN enables: Real-time recommendations Large catalog retrieval Personalized ranking Exact search here would introduce unacceptable latency at scale. C. Image & Multimedia Retrieval Image search systems convert visuals into embeddings capturing: Shapes Textures Objects Context When you search using an image (reverse image search), ANN retrieves visually similar embeddings. This powers: Stock photo discovery Visual product search Facial similarity systems Medical imaging comparisons Again, the dataset sizes make exact search impractical. D. Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) One of the most visible modern use cases. RAG pipelines work like this: User query → embedding Knowledge base → vectorized ANN retrieval → fetch relevant context LLM → generates answer Here, ANN directly affects: Response relevance Hallucination reduction Context grounding And because LLM responses are user-facing, retrieval latency must remain low — making ANN essential. E. Fraud & Anomaly Detection In financial and security systems, embeddings represent: Transactions Behavioral patterns Device fingerprints ANN helps detect: Similar fraud patterns Behavioral anomalies Suspicious clusters Here, recall requirements are often higher — but ANN still provides the speed needed for near-real-time monitoring. Why ANN Is Non-Negotiable in Production Across these domains, a few patterns repeat: Datasets are massive Embeddings are high-dimensional Queries must be real-time Ranking quality matters Exact search can deliver accuracy… …but ANN delivers usability. Without ANN: Semantic search would lag Recommendations would batch offline RAG systems would stall Image retrieval would be slow In many ways, ANN is the invisible infrastructure that makes AI applications feel instantaneous. The Engineer’s Realization This is the point where ANN stops feeling like an academic optimization… …and starts feeling like a foundational system primitive. Just like: Indexes in relational databases Caches in distributed systems CDNs in web delivery ANN becomes a default expectation in any embedding-driven architecture.
Preview of ANN Algorithms Up until now, we’ve spoken about Approximate Nearest Neighbor search mostly in conceptual terms. We said systems: Partition space Navigate neighborhoods Prune irrelevant regions Balance recall vs latency But naturally, the engineer in me wanted to know: “What are the actual algorithms doing this work?” Because approximation doesn’t come from a single technique. It comes from an ecosystem of indexing strategies — each with different tradeoffs in: Speed Accuracy Memory usage Index build time Let’s briefly preview the major families you’ll encounter inside vector databases. A. Graph-Based Indexes (HNSW) One of the most widely used ANN approaches today. Core idea: Vectors form a navigable graph where: Each node connects to similar neighbors Multiple layers exist for hierarchical navigation Search moves from coarse → fine regions Query search looks like: Enter graph from top layer Traverse toward closer nodes Descend layers for refinement Why it’s popular: Extremely high recall Fast query latency Strong real-world performance You’ll see HNSW inside systems like: Qdrant Weaviate Milvus Elasticsearch vector search We’ll deep dive into this later — especially graph construction and traversal. B. Inverted File Index (IVF) IVF takes a clustering approach. Core idea: Partition vectors into clusters (centroids) Store vectors within cluster “lists” At query time: Find nearest centroids Search only within those clusters So search becomes: Cluster selection → Local similarity search Benefits: Faster than brute force Memory efficient Tunable via number of clusters probed Tradeoff: Lower recall than graph-based methods if under-probed C. Product Quantization (PQ) PQ optimizes for memory and speed. Instead of storing full vectors, it: Compresses vectors into smaller codes Quantizes subspaces Approximates distances via lookup tables This enables: Massive storage reduction Faster distance computation Billion-scale indexing on limited hardware Tradeoff: Some loss in precision More complex indexing pipeline Often combined with IVF (IVF-PQ). D. Locality-Sensitive Hashing (LSH) One of the earlier ANN approaches. Core idea: Hash vectors so that: Similar vectors land in the same buckets Dissimilar ones land far apart Search becomes: Hash query vector Retrieve candidates from same bucket Benefits: Fast lookup Theoretically elegant Limitations: Lower recall Less dominant in modern vector DBs Still relevant in specific high-speed retrieval contexts. Why So Many Algorithms Exist Because ANN optimization isn’t one-dimensional. Different workloads prioritize different things: Vector databases often support multiple index types — or hybrid combinations — to adapt to use cases. Setting Up What Comes Next This preview serves two purposes: It demystifies ANN — showing it’s not magic It prepares us for implementation deep dives Because once we start exploring open-source vector DBs (like Qdrant), we’ll see these algorithms directly in: Index structures Configuration parameters Storage layouts Query execution paths So understanding their intuition now makes system internals much easier to grasp later.
How Vector Databases Use ANN As we move from the theoretical foundations of Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) algorithms, it’s important to understand where they are actually used in real-world systems. One of the most impactful adopters of ANN today is the Vector Database. Why Vector Databases Need ANN Modern AI applications — especially those powered by embeddings — operate in high-dimensional vector space. Examples: Text embeddings (LLMs, semantic search) Image embeddings (CLIP, vision models) Audio embeddings Recommendation vectors Each object is stored as something like: Now imagine searching similarity across: Millions Or even billions of such vectors. Running exact nearest neighbor search here would require: Computing distance against every vector Extremely high latency Massive compute cost This is where ANN becomes essential. ANN as the Search Engine of Vector DBs Vector databases use ANN algorithms as their core indexing and retrieval engine. Instead of brute force comparison, ANN structures the vector space so that: Similar vectors are clustered Search paths are optimized Irrelevant regions are skipped This enables: Sub-second similarity search Scalable retrieval Real-time AI applications How the Flow Works Here’s a simplified lifecycle of ANN inside a vector database:
Embedding Generation Data is converted into vectors using ML models. Example: Sentence → 768-dim vector Image → 1024-dim vector
ANN Index Construction The database builds ANN structures such as: HNSW graphs IVF clusters PQ compressed indexes These act like multi-layer navigation maps of vector space.
Query Vector Search When a query comes in: Query → embedding vector ANN index is traversed Closest candidates are shortlisted
Similarity Retrieval Top-K nearest vectors are returned based on: Cosine similarity Euclidean distance Dot product Popular Ve,ctor Databases Using ANN Some well-known implementations include: FAISS (Meta) Milvus Pinecone Weaviate Qdrant Elasticsearch (vector search) OpenSearch Each uses different ANN strategies depending on: Scale Accuracy needs Latency targets Summary In this part, we moved beyond theory and explored how Approximate Nearest Neighbor (ANN) algorithms power real-world vector search systems. We began by understanding why traditional exact nearest neighbor search becomes impractical in high-dimensional spaces — especially when dealing with millions or billions of embeddings generated from text, images, or other AI workloads. From there, we saw how ANN algorithms solve this challenge by introducing structured indexing techniques that make similarity search both fast and scalable. Instead of brute-force distance comparisons, ANN organizes vectors using graphs, clusters, and compression strategies to quickly navigate toward the most relevant neighbors. We then connected these concepts to vector databases, where ANN acts as the core retrieval engine. We walked through the lifecycle: Embedding generation ANN index construction Query vector traversal Top-K similarity retrieval This highlighted how modern systems enable sub-second semantic search, recommendation engines, and Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) pipelines. We also touched on the practical trade-off between accuracy and latency, and how tuning ANN parameters allows systems to balance retrieval quality with performance. Now that we’ve built a conceptual foundation around ANN and vector databases, the next part will shift from theory to practice. We’ll look at: How to generate embeddings How ANN indexes are created How similarity search works in code A simple end-to-end vector search example This is where the concepts come alive — turning mathematical intuition into working implementations.

Source: 2026-05-31 8 Vector Indexes: Cost vs Recall Showdown | by Nexumo | Medium
Type: Pasted Text
WebSync metadata title: 8 Vector Indexes: Cost vs Recall Showdown | by Nexumo | Medium url: https://medium.com/@Nexumo_/8-vector-indexes-cost-vs-recall-showdown-d61f0fac4f0a date: 2026-05-31T00:45:26.803Z parsing method: defuddle Sitemap Compare 8 popular vector indexes — Flat, HNSW, IVF, PQ, ScaNN, DiskANN, and more — through the lens of cost vs recall. Snippets and buyer-style advice included. Vector search looks simple — until you have a million embeddings and a deadline. You need great recall. You also need low cost. And you absolutely need something you can ship without a month of parameter roulette. This piece cuts the noise: eight indexes, what they cost, the recall you should expect, and when each is the right call. What “Cost” Really Means Here When teams say cost, they usually mean a bundle: Memory footprint: RAM (and sometimes SSD) per million vectors. Latency: p50/p95 query time at k=10 (ballpark guidance). Index build time: minutes → hours. Ops overhead: tuning complexity and failure modes (OOM, slow rebuilds). We’ll assume d=768 vectors, n=1M, and k=10 as a mental model. Your mileage will vary, but the trade-offs hold. TL;DR Table (Rules of Thumb) Let’s be real: exact percentages depend on data distribution and hyperparameters. Treat these as ranges, not promises.
Flat (Exact) — The Gold Standard Baseline When to use: n ≤ 200k, or you’re benchmarking new embeddings. You need perfect recall for evaluation or critical workflows. Why it’s great: Zero surprises, zero drift. If Flat is too slow/expensive, you now have a concrete target to beat.
HNSW — Graph Speed, Memory Price Why teams love it: Ridiculously good recall/latency with sane tuning (M, efConstruction, efSearch). Great for recommendation and semantic search when RAM is plentiful. Costs: Memory: each vector stores multiple neighbor links; RAM climbs quickly at scale. Build: parallelizable but not trivial at 100M+. Use when: You want near-exact quality and can pay in RAM.
IVF-Flat — The Workhorse Idea: Partition the space into nlist clusters (k-means). At query time, probe only nprobe clusters and run exact search within them. Trade-off knob: Increase nprobe → higher recall, higher latency. Why it’s nice: Memory scales well; tuning is intuitive.
IVF-PQ — Your First Big RAM Win Idea: Same IVF, but store compressed Product Quantization (PQ) codes in each list instead of raw float vectors. Rule of thumb: m codebooks × nbits bits each → m * nbits / 8 bytes per vector. Example: m=64, nbits=8 ⇒ 64 bytes/vector (vs 3KB for 768×float32). Caveat: Aggressive PQ reduces recall. Start conservative (more bytes), then squeeze.
OPQ + IVF-PQ — Make PQ Smarter What changes: Optimized PQ (OPQ) rotates the space before quantization to minimize distortion; you keep the RAM savings while recovering recall. When it shines: Multimodal embeddings or skewed dimensions where plain PQ hurts. Cost: Extra training step and slightly longer build times. Usually worth it.
ScaNN — Hash, Score, Re-rank Pitch: Google’s approach mixes asymmetric hashing / partitioning and a tiny exact re-rank over candidates. It’s fast and friendly to both CPU and accelerator paths. Why teams pick it: Strong recall at low latency on common cloud VMs. Good fit when you want “great out of the box,” not just “good after 50 parameter sweeps.” Watch for: Tuning still matters (tree shards, hash parameters), but defaults are surprisingly sane.
DiskANN / Vamana — SSD-First Indexing Design: Graph-based ANN that keeps a minimal RAM resident set and reads neighbors from NVMe on demand. Result: Million-scale datasets with desktop-scale RAM. Why it matters: If your CFO’s first question is “how much RAM?”, DiskANN answers with “less than you fear.” Costs: More complex build pipeline and careful SSD provisioning. Latency remains low with good NVMe.
Annoy — Dead-Simple, Battle-Tested What it is: Forests of random projection trees. Why it’s loved: Dead easy to use, predictable, okay recall, persistent indexes on disk. Use when: You need something stable today, your dataset isn’t huge, and latency targets are modest. Choosing Under Real Constraints If RAM is the wall Pick: IVF-PQ, OPQ+IVF-PQ, or DiskANN. Why: They slash memory without tanking recall. Tip: Start with OPQ+IVF-PQ and store 64–96 bytes/vector; measure recall with a held-out exact baseline. If latency is king Pick: HNSW or ScaNN. Why: Both deliver excellent p95 with thoughtful tuning. Tip: For HNSW, raise efSearch for recall until you hit your latency ceiling. If simplicity beats everything Pick: Flat (small n) or Annoy (medium n). Why: Minimal tuning; predictable ops. If your dataset will explode Pick: IVF-PQ now, with a migration path to DiskANN later. Why: You’ll learn your distribution while keeping costs tolerable. Tiny Math That Saves Budget Meetings Approximate RAM for raw storage:n * d * 4 bytes For 1M × 768×fp32 ≈ 2.9 GB (vectors only). With PQ ( m=64, nbits=8):n * 64 * 1 = 64 MB plus codebooks and IVF lists. Even after overhead, you’re often under 0.2–0.4 GB for the payload—an order of magnitude smaller. You might be wondering: “But doesn’t PQ hurt reranking?” If you keep raw vectors for only the top-k or   store a smaller fp16 cache for candidates, you recover most quality with tiny incremental cost. A Minimal Evaluation Harness (Do This First) Why this matters: You’ll stop arguing in abstracts and pick the index that hits your recall/latency target on your data. Buyer-Style Recommendations Start with a baseline. Build Flat first to lock in an exact recall target. Pick a family, not a brand. Whether you’re in FAISS, ScaNN, USearch, Qdrant, Milvus, or Vespa, the index family (graph, IVF, PQ, SSD-graph) dictates 90% of behavior. Tune one knob at a time. For IVF: tune nlist (build) then nprobe (query). For HNSW: set M, efConstruction, then sweep efSearch. Measure p95, not just p50. Tail latency kills UX. Plan for drift. New embeddings change geometry; schedule light re-training (IVF, OPQ) and monitor recall. Conclusion There’s no single “best” vector index — only the one that hits your cost and your recall on your data. If you want plug-and-play excellence and have RAM, go HNSW. If you need scale on a budget, go OPQ + IVF-PQ. If SSD is cheap and RAM isn’t, DiskANN is your friend. And never skip the Flat baseline — it’s the compass that keeps your tuning honest. CTA: Tell me your target recall, latency, and memory budget in the comments, and I’ll suggest a concrete index + parameter set you can try this week. Next-move engineering for builders - fast, clear, measurable. Systems, data, and AI you can ship today.

Payment & Billing FAQ: Integrations FAQ: Security Version: User Guides (Cloud) On this page ANN Search Explained file_copy Copy page A k-nearest neighbor (kNN) search finds the k-nearest vectors to a query vector. Specifically, it compares a query vector to every vector in a vector space until k exact matches appear. Although kNN searches guarantee perfect accuracy, they are time-consuming, especially for large datasets comprising high-dimensional vectors. In contrast, approximate nearest neighbor (ANN) searches require building an index beforehand. Various indexing algorithms demonstrate trade-offs among search speed, memory usage, and accuracy. Generally, two paths are available to implement these algorithms: narrowing the search scope and decomposing high-dimensional vector spaces into low-dimensional subspaces. Narrowing the search scope can reduce search time by selecting only a subset of possible candidates for comparison with the query vector. This avoids irrelevant vectors. To determine whether a vector is in the subset, an index structure is needed to sort the vectors. There are generally three ideas available for forming the index structure: graphs, trees, and hashes. HNSW: A graph-based indexing algorithm Hierarchical Navigable Small World (HNSW) indexes a vector space by creating a hierarchical proximity graph. Specifically, HNSW draws proximity links (or edges) between vectors (or vertices) on each layer to form a single-layer proximity graph and stacks them up to form the hierarchical graph. The bottom layer holds all vectors and their proximity links. As the layer goes up, only a smaller set of vectors and proximity links remains. Once the hierarchical proximity graph is created, the search goes as follows: Find a vector as the entry point on the top layer. Move gradually to the nearest vector along the available proximity links. Once you determine the nearest vector at the top layer, use the same vector at a lower layer as the entry point to find its nearest neighbor at that layer. Repeat the preceding steps until you find the nearest vector at the bottom layer. LSH: A hash-based ANN indexing algorithm Locality-sensitive hashing (LSH) indexes a vector space by mapping data pieces of any length to fixed-length values as hashes using various hash functions, gathering these hashes into hash buckets, and tagging vectors that have been hashed to the same value at least once as candidate pairs. DiskANN: ANN search on disk based on Vamana graphs Unlike HNSW that builds a hierarchical graph for layered searches, Vamana's indexing process is relatively simple: Initialize a random graph; Find the navigation point by first locating the global centroid and determining the closest point. Use a global comparison to minimize the average search radius. Perform Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search with the initialized random neighbor graph and search starting point from step 2. Use all points on the search path as candidate neighbor sets and apply the edge trimming strategy with alpha = 1 to reduce the search radius. Repeat step 3 with adjusted alpha > 1 (1.2 recommended in the paper) to improve graph quality and recall rate. Once the index is ready, the search goes as follows: Load relevant data, including query set, PQ center point data, codebook data, search starting point, and index meta. Use the indexed data set to perform cached_beam_search, count the access times of each point, and cache the num_nodes_to_cache points with the highest access frequency. WARMUP operation is performed by default using the sample data set to perform a cached_beam_search. Perform cached_beam_search with the query set for each given parameter L, and output statistics such as recall rate and QPS. Warmup and hotspot data statistics are not included in query time. For details, refer to DiskANN, A Disk-based ANNS Solution with High Recall and High QPS on Billion-scale Dataset. Source: BANG: Billion-Scale Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search using a Single GPU - arXiv

BANG: Billion-Scale Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search using a Single GPU Back to arXiv

License: arXiv.org perpetual non-exclusive license arXiv:2401.11324v3 [cs.DC] 22 Jan 2025 BANG: Billion-Scale Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search using a Single GPU Report issue for preceding element Karthik V., Saim Khan, Somesh Singh, Harsha Vardhan Simhadri and Jyothi Vedurada ∙ ∙ \bullet ∙ Karthik V., Saim Khan and Jyothi Vedurada are with IIT Hyderabad, India. ∙ ∙ \bullet ∙ Harsha Vardhan Simhadri is with Microsoft Research, USA. ∙ ∙ \bullet ∙ Somesh Singh is with LabEx MILYON and LIP (UMR5668), France. Report issue for preceding element Abstract Report issue for preceding element Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search (ANNS) is a subroutine in algorithms routinely employed in information retrieval, pattern recognition, data mining, image processing, and beyond. Recent works have established that graph-based ANNS algorithms are practically more efficient than the other methods proposed in the literature. The growing volume and dimensionality of data necessitates designing scalable techniques for ANNS. To this end, the prior art has explored parallelizing graph-based ANNS on GPU leveraging its massive parallelism. The current state-of-the-art GPU-based ANNS algorithms either (i) require both the dataset and the generated graph index to reside entirely in the GPU memory, or (ii) they partition the dataset into small independent shards, each of which can fit in GPU memory, and perform the search on these shards on the GPU. While the first approach fails to handle large datasets due to the limited memory available on the GPU, the latter delivers poor performance on large datasets due to high data traffic over the low-bandwidth PCIe bus. Report issue for preceding element We introduce BANG, a first-of-its-kind technique for graph-based ANNS on GPU for billion-scale datasets, that cannot entirely fit in the GPU memory. BANG stands out by harnessing a compressed form of the dataset on a single GPU to perform distance computations while efficiently accessing the graph index kept on the host memory, enabling efficient ANNS on large graphs within the limited GPU memory. BANG incorporates highly-optimized GPU kernels and proceeds in phases that run concurrently on the GPU and CPU, taking advantage of their architectural specificities. Furthermore, it enables overlapping communication with computation that results in efficient data transfer between the CPU and GPU. We evaluate BANG using a single NVIDIA Ampere A100 GPU on three popular ANN benchmark datasets. BANG outperforms the state-of-the-art comprehensively. Notably, on the billion-size datasets, we achieve throughputs 30 × \times × -200 × \times × more than the competing methods for a high recall value of 0.9. Report issue for preceding element Index Terms: Report issue for preceding element Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search, Graph and tree search strategies, Information retrieval, Approximate search, vector similarity search, GPU, Big data. I Introduction Report issue for preceding element The k 𝑘 k italic_k -Nearest-Neighbour-Search problem is to find the k 𝑘 k italic_k nearest data points to a given query point in a multidimensional dataset. As the dimensionality increases, exact search methods become increasingly inefficient. In order to evaluate the exact k 𝑘 k italic_k -nearest-neighbours of a query point in a d 𝑑 d italic_d -dimensional dataset having n 𝑛 n italic_n points, as a consequence of the curse of dimensionality, all the n 𝑛 n italic_n points must be examined; this takes O  ( n  d ) 𝑂 𝑛 𝑑 O(nd) italic_O ( italic_n italic_d ) time [ 28] . Therefore, it is common practice to use Approximate Nearest Neighbour Search (ANNS) when looking for nearest neighbours in order to mitigate the curse of dimensionality by sacrificing a small accuracy for speed [ 68] . ANNS accuracy is typically measured as recall, which quantifies the overlap between the retrieved nearest neighbours and the ground truth for a query. ANNS is widely applied in information retrieval [ 10, 7, 39] , recommendation systems [ 48, 57] , search engines [ 65] , and computer vision [ 69] to search large datasets of words, documents, images, and multimedia. Advances in deep learning [ 6] have made ANNS essential for similarity search [ 68] , including RAG [ 44] . The use of ANNS in VDBMS [ 56] is vital for query processing in unstructured data applications like e-commerce [ 46] . Search queries on such massive multidimensional datasets are often processed in batches to meet the high throughput demands, as evidenced by recent efforts [ 31, 25, 63, 67] leveraging the massive parallelism of GPUs for ANNS. Application of k 𝑘 k italic_k nearest neighbour ( k 𝑘 k italic_k NN) query in road networks, e.g. finding the k 𝑘 k italic_k nearest Uber cars of a user for ride-sharing, was one early application of GPUs for ANNS [ 34] . Report issue for preceding element Billion-scale ANNS is computationally intensive, with pairwise distance calculations well-suited for GPU architectures. Zhang et al. [ 66] compared CPU-based and GPU-based ANNS techniques, concluding that GPUs offer higher performance and cost-effectiveness for accelerating vector query processing. The Billion-Scale Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search Challenge [ 52] evaluated ANNS on various hardware platforms, showing that GPU implementations significantly outperformed baselines in both throughput and recall. Billion-scale datasets exceed single GPU memory, requiring multiple GPUs [ 25] , which introduces two challenges: (a) a significant escalation in implementation cost (as seen in the Track3 Cost/Power Leaderboards [ 52] ), (b) a mismatch between GPU compute and memory resources. Report issue for preceding element Graph-based ANNS algorithms [ 36, 22, 29] have been shown to be generally more efficient in practice at handling large datasets. However, GPU implementations of these algorithms [ 45, 67, 25] require storing graph data structures in GPU memory, which limits their ability to handle large datasets. Even recent GPUs like the NVIDIA Ampere A100 having 80GB device memory, cannot accommodate the entire input data (graph index and data points). Prior solutions, such as sharding, effectively implemented by GGNN [ 25] , result in high memory transfer costs. For example, even with the PCIe 4.0 interconnect operating at its peak theoretical transfer rate of 32 GB/s, transferring the DEEP1B dataset (consisting of a data size of 384GB and a graph index size of 260GB) from CPU to GPU would take 20 seconds, resulting in a low throughput of less than 500 QPS for 10,000 concurrent queries on the GPU. Alternatively, hashing and compression techniques, as showcased by SONG [ 67] and FAISS [ 31] , can handle large volumes of data using a single GPU by reducing the data dimensionality or by compressing the vectors. However, these approaches have limitations when it comes to high recalls for massive datasets. It has been shown that it is feasible to attain high throughputs at high recalls on large datasets using multiple GPUs [ 25] . However, these approaches have a very high hardware cost which makes them undesirable. Thus, this paper explores an important question: Can we increase the throughput of ANNS queries without compromising their recall by using a single GPU? Report issue for preceding element In this paper, we introduce BANG, a novel GPU-based ANNS method, which is designed to efficiently handle large datasets, with billions of points, that cannot entirely fit in GPU memory. BANG stands out by employing compressed data for accelerated distance computations on the GPU while keeping the graph index and the dataset on the CPU, thus enabling efficient ANNS on large datasets. CPU transfers neighbour information to the GPU on demand in every iteration of the search. Furthermore, in contrast to the previous approaches that treat search activity as one monolithic block, BANG divides ANNS activity into distinct phases, inorder to maximize resource utilization and mitigate CPU-GPU data transfer bottlenecks. Specifically, the division into phases leads to three advantages: (1) executing different phases on CPU and GPU in a pipeline (2) optimizing each phase's (GPU kernel's) span characteristics separately to maximize parallelism on GPU (3) asynchronously prefetching required data from CPU to GPU for specific phases. Thus, the overall performance of BANG stems from several factors, such as CPU-GPU load balancing, highly optimised GPU kernels (for tasks such as distance calculations, sorting, and updating worklists), prefetching and pipelining. Report issue for preceding element ANNS methods typically follow the index and search paradigm whereby data points are first processed to construct an efficient index, and search queries are then processed using this index. In this work, our focus is efficient Billion-scale ANNS on GPU utilizing an underlying graph index, and therefore we do not construct a graph index but instead utilize an existing one from prior work [ 29] . Report issue for preceding element Our evaluations show that BANG significantly outperforms the state-of-the-art on four popular real-world billion-size ANN benchmark datasets with varying characteristics across all recall values, on a single NVIDIA A100 GPU. Notably, on these datasets we achieve 30 × \times × -200 × \times × higher throughput than the competing methods for a high recall of 0.9. Report issue for preceding element This paper makes the following main contributions: Report issue for preceding element • We introduce BANG, a novel GPU-based ANNS method for efficiently searching billion-scale datasets using a single GPU. Report issue for preceding element • We propose a phased-execution strategy that enables pipelined execution of ANNS steps on both CPU and GPU, optimizing CPU-GPU load balancing, asynchronous data transfer, and GPU kernel performance for maximizing parallelism. Report issue for preceding element • We present efficient GPU kernels for distance calculations and worklist updates in ANNS and implement prefetching and pipelining to effectively utilize CPU and GPU, reducing traffic over PCIe interconnect and enhancing throughput while retaining recall. Report issue for preceding element • We conduct an extensive evaluation demonstrating that BANG significantly outperforms state-of-the-art GPU-based ANNS methods in terms of throughput and cost. Report issue for preceding element II Background Report issue for preceding element II-A GPU Architecture and Programming Model Report issue for preceding element Graphic Processor Units (GPUs) are accelerators that offer massive multi-threading and high memory bandwidth [ 27] . CPU ( host) and GPU ( device) are connected via an interconnect such as PCI Express that supports CPU ⇄ ⇄ \rightleftarrows ⇄ GPU communications. For our implementation, we use NVIDIA GPU with the CUDA [ 4] programming model. The main memory space of GPU is referred to as global memory. A GPU comprises several CUDA cores, which are organized into streaming multiprocessors (SMs). All cores of a SM share on-chip shared memory, which is a software-managed L1 data cache. GPU procedures, referred to as kernels, run on the SMs in parallel by launching a grid of threads. Threads are grouped into thread-blocks, with each block assigned to a SM. Threads within a thread-block communicate and synchronize via shared memory. A thread-block comprises warps, each containing 32 threads, executing in a single-instruction-multiple-data (SIMD) fashion. Kernel invocation and memory transfers are performed using task queues called streams, which can be used for task parallelism. Report issue for preceding element II-B Graph Index Report issue for preceding element Many top-performing ANNS algorithms are graph-based, achieving both high recall and high throughput, measured as queries per second (QPS). A graph-based ANNS algorithm runs on a proximity graph, which is pre-constructed over the dataset points by connecting each point with nearby points. Our BANG algorithm implements an efficient ANNS that can run on various proximity graphs such as kNN [ 25] , HNSW [ 36] , Vamana [ 29] etc. Manohar et al. [ 38] conducted a study on various CPU-based ANNS algorithms and showed that DiskANN [ 29] approach that runs on the Vamana graph index achieves superior throughput and recall on large datasets. Furthermore, the recent work by Wang et al. [ 59] , which proposes enhancements to the Vamana graph index for disk-resident indexes, also highlights the significance of the Vamana graph. Hence, we utilized the Vamana graph as the underlying search structure in the implementation of BANG. Vamana graph index is constructed by sharding the large dataset into smaller overlapping clusters. The technique iteratively processes individual clusters and uses RobustPrune and GreedySearch routines to construct directed sub-graphs on host RAM, which are then stored on SSD. The final graph is formed by uniting the edges in the sub-graphs. While prior techniques (that construct the graph index using the SNG property [ 5] ) aim to reduce distances to the query point along the search path, Vamana further reduces hops to the query point with the RobustPrune property, resulting in a fast search. Report issue for preceding element III ANN Search on GPU: Challenges in Handling Billion-scale Data Report issue for preceding element We propose BANG, a method for parallel ANNS on a single GPU. There are two main challenges in parallelizing ANNS on a single GPU: (1) managing large graphs within the constraints of limited GPU memory, (2) extracting sufficient parallelism for ANNS to fully utilize the hardware resources. In the following subsections, we discuss these challenges in detail. Report issue for preceding element III-A Limited GPU Memory Report issue for preceding element A primary challenge when dealing with large datasets on GPUs is their massive memory footprint. Table I shows the sizes of various billion-scale ANNS datasets and their respective graph sizes. Notably, even with the recent A100 GPU's maximum global memory capacity of 80GB, it is evident that the entire input data (base, graph, and query) cannot be accommodated within the GPU memory. To address this challenge, a recent work GGNN [ 25] effectively implemented sharding as a solution. However, this approach requires frequent swaps of both processed and unprocessed shards between GPU and host RAM, resulting in high memory transfer costs over the PCI interconnect. Further, these swaps may result in an idle time of CPU/GPU, and optimal GPU-CPU coordination is essential during iterative graph traversals of ANNS. Furthermore, sharding the entire data is not viable because even with the PCIe 4.0 interconnect operating at its peak theoretical transfer bandwidth of 32 GB/s, it will take an estimated 20 seconds to transfer the largest dataset from CPU to GPU and will result in a markedly low throughput of less than 500 QPS as explained in Section I. Report issue for preceding element Although employing a multi-GPU setup can facilitate the effective distribution of shards across all GPUs to store the entire input data (as demonstrated with eight GPUs in GGNN [ 25] ), this approach incurs significant hardware cost. As a result, our focus is on developing an efficient and cost-effective parallel implementation of ANNS on a single GPU. Report issue for preceding element TABLE I: Sizes of Data and Graphs for Various Datasets. Report issue for preceding element Alternatively, hashing and quantization techniques can handle large data on a single GPU. Hashing effectively reduces data dimensionality to make it fit within GPU memory, as demonstrated by SONG [ 67] , which compressed a 784-byte vector to 64 bytes in the MNIST8M dataset. However, hashing is better suited for smaller datasets and may not achieve high recall on datasets with billions of points. Data compression, as demonstrated by FAISS [ 31] , can accelerate query processing in GPU-based ANNS, but it cannot provide high recall. Report issue for preceding element To remedy these limitations, we present a novel GPU-based solution wherein we conduct ANNS that does not require the large graph to be present on GPU memory, instead the CPU retrieves the required neighbourhood information from the graph index kept on the host RAM and transfers it to GPU efficiently. Likewise, since the GPU cannot accommodate the entire base dataset, the search process on the GPU employs compressed vectors (instead of the base dataset vectors) for distance calculations. The compressed vectors are generated using the widely utilized Product Quantization [ 32] technique, employed in numerous previous works, including FAISS [ 31] and DiskANN [ 29] . Report issue for preceding element Thus BANG mitigates the CPU-GPU data transfer bottleneck by transferring only compressed data to the GPU and by eliminating the need for transferring the entire graph to the GPU by fetching the neighbour information for a node from CPU to GPU on demand. Report issue for preceding element III-B Optimal Hardware Usage Report issue for preceding element BANG's approach of obtaining neighbours from the CPU and calculating distances from them to the query on the GPU using compressed vectors presents two challenges. Report issue for preceding element The primary challenge is to prevent CPU and GPU idleness by ensuring their concurrent and continuous utilization despite the dependence on neighbour information between these devices. Furthermore, it is important to consider the volume of data that needs to be transferred between the CPU and GPU when balancing the work distribution between them to ensure that performance is not constrained by bandwidth limitations caused by prolonged data transfers, as in the GGNN [ 25] framework. To address these issues, we propose a parallel ANNS implementation BANG that extracts maximum parallelism by efficiently utilizing the hardware (CPU, GPU and PCIe bus) with a phased-execution approach to efficiently load balance the ANNS work across CPU and GPU and to minimize CPU-GPU data transfer bottlenecks. The phased-execution strategy facilitates the execution of different steps of ANNS in a pipeline on both CPU and GPU, which enables: (1) CPU-GPU load balancing to minimize CPU and GPU idle time, (2) using prefetching techniques to transfer data from CPU to GPU asynchronously for certain phases, and (3) optimizing the span characteristics of each GPU kernel (corresponding to operations such as distance computation, sorting and updating worklist) separately to maximize parallelism. Report issue for preceding element The second challenge is to optimize data structure placement according to computational strengths and memory access capabilities of the CPU and GPU, owing to the higher memory footprint caused by thousands of queries running concurrently on the data in the GPU. For instance, when handling parallel queries (Q) on the GPU and managing data structures for tracking visited nodes (V) and their processing, in order to determine whether structures should be placed on the CPU or GPU, several key factors need to be considered, such as the computational complexity associated with the memory size of Q × V 𝑄 𝑉 Q\times V italic_Q × italic_V , the patterns of memory accesses within V (access times vary with CPU and GPU memory bandwidths), and the time it takes to transfer data Q × V 𝑄 𝑉 Q\times V italic_Q × italic_V given the limited bandwidth of the PCIe interconnect between the CPU and GPU. BANG handles this by using an efficient implementation of bloom filter [ 8] on the GPU. We discuss this in detail in Section IV-C. Report issue for preceding element IV BANG: Billion-scale ANNS on a Single GPU Report issue for preceding element Given a set of available queries, 𝒬 𝒬 \mathcal{Q} caligraphic_Q , BANG processes the queries in parallel by effectively using CPU (host), GPU (device) and PCIe (data transfer link between CPU and GPU), achieving high throughput on billion-scale data using a single GPU. BANG is a graph-based ANNS technique.
